2:22:1271:0:14:72:326:700:12:Times:12:Times:C:The Nature of Credit Constraints and Human Capital
2:23:1321:14:14:72:308:686:12:Times:12:Times:C:Lance J. Lochner and Alexander Monge-Naranjo
2:24:1365:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:C:NBER Working Paper No. 13912
2:25:1393:14:14:72:124:658:12:Times:12:Times:C:April 2008
2:26:1403:14:28:72:174:644:12:Times:12:Times:C:JEL No. H81,I22,I28
2:27:1422:28:28:306:339:616:12:Times:12:Times:C:ABSTRACT
2:28:1430:28:14:72:540:588:12:Times:12:Times:F:This paper studies the nature and impact of credit constraints in the market for human capital. We
2:29:1528:14:14:72:540:574:12:Times:12:Times:F:derive endogenous constraints from the design of government student loan programs and from the
2:30:1622:14:14:72:540:560:12:Times:12:Times:F:limited repayment incentives in private lending markets. These constraints imply cross-sectional patterns
2:31:1727:14:14:72:540:546:12:Times:12:Times:F:for schooling, ability, and family income that are consistent with U.S. data. This contrasts with the
2:32:1828:14:14:72:540:532:12:Times:12:Times:F:standard exogenous constraint model, which predicts a counterfactual negative ability -- schooling
2:33:1926:14:14:72:540:518:12:Times:12:Times:F:relationship for low-income youth. We show that the rising empirical importance of familial wealth
2:34:2024:14:14:72:540:504:12:Times:12:Times:F:and income in determining college attendance (Belley and Lochner  2007) is consistent with increasingly
2:35:2127:14:14:72:540:490:12:Times:12:Times:F:binding credit constraints in the face of rising tuition costs and returns to schooling. Our framework
2:36:2229:14:14:72:540:476:12:Times:12:Times:F:also explains the recent increase in private credit for college as a market response to the rising returns
2:37:2335:14:42:72:119:462:12:Times:12:Times:C:to school.
2:38:2345:42:14:72:155:420:12:Times:12:Times:C:Lance J. Lochner
2:39:2361:14:14:72:326:406:12:Times:12:Times:C:Department of Economics, Faculty of Social Science
2:40:2411:14:14:72:218:392:12:Times:12:Times:C:University of Western Ontario
2:41:2440:14:14:72:214:378:12:Times:12:Times:C:1151 Richmond Street, North
2:42:2467:14:14:72:185:364:12:Times:12:Times:C:London, ON  N6A 5C2
2:43:2486:14:14:72:108:350:12:Times:12:Times:C:Canada
2:44:2492:14:14:72:124:336:12:Times:12:Times:C:and NBER
2:45:2500:14:28:72:157:322:12:Times:12:Times:C:llochner@uwo.ca
2:46:2515:28:14:72:202:294:12:Times:12:Times:C:Alexander Monge-Naranjo
2:47:2538:14:14:72:198:280:12:Times:12:Times:C:Department of Economics
2:48:2561:14:14:72:191:266:12:Times:12:Times:C:Northwestern University
2:49:2584:14:14:72:169:252:12:Times:12:Times:C:Andersen Hall 3232
2:50:2602:14:14:72:170:238:12:Times:12:Times:C:Evanston, IL  60208
2:51:2621:14:0:72:187:224:12:Times:12:Times:C:alexmonge@gmail.com
3:52:2640:0:30:72:208:775:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:1  Introduction
3:53:2655:30:18:72:540:745:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Borrowing constraints have a long history in the literature on schooling and human capital
3:54:2745:18:18:72:540:727:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investment. Because human capital cannot be repossessed in response to default, it makes
3:55:2833:18:18:72:540:709:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for poor collateral. Since investing in human capital is most efficient when individuals are
3:56:2925:18:18:72:540:691:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:young, most students have not established a credit reputation or accumulated other forms
3:57:3013:18:18:72:540:673:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of collateral. As a result, private financial institutions have historically offered little credit
3:58:3111:18:18:72:540:655:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to finance higher education.1Even when governments have stepped in with public student
3:59:3197:18:18:72:540:637:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lending programs, the credit offered through these programs has been quite limited, and
3:60:3284:18:18:72:484:619:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:credit constraints may still play an important role in higher education decisions.
3:61:3366:18:18:89:539:601:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As pointed out by Becker (1975), youth with few family resources under-invest in their
3:62:3452:18:18:72:540:583:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:human capital if they cannot obtain adequate credit.  This observation has motivated a
3:63:3538:18:18:72:540:565:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:voluminous literature examining the relationship between family income (or wealth) and
3:64:3624:18:17:72:540:547:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:college attendance (e.g. Manski and Wise 1983, Cameron and Heckman 1998, 2001, Ellwood
3:65:3710:17:18:72:540:530:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and Kane 2000, Carneiro and Heckman 2002, Belley and Lochner 2007). Using U.S. data
3:66:3793:18:18:72:540:512:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from the 1979 and 1997 cohorts of the National Longitudinal Survey of Youth (NLSY79
3:67:3876:18:18:72:540:494:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and NLSY97, respectively) and controlling for individual differences in ability and family
3:68:3966:18:18:72:540:476:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:background, Belley and Lochner (2007) estimate a weak family income – college attendance
3:69:4054:18:18:72:539:458:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:relationship in the early 1980s but a much stronger positive relationship in the early 2000s.2
3:70:4148:18:18:72:540:440:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This seems to suggest that borrowing constraints had little effect on schooling decisions in the
3:71:4244:18:18:72:540:422:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:early 1980s, but that their effects have grown more important in recent years. Consistent with
3:72:4338:18:18:72:540:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this hypothesis, recent U.S. Department of Education studies (Berkner 2000 and Titus 2002)
3:73:4428:18:18:72:540:386:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:report that the fraction of undergraduate student borrowers who borrowed the maximum
3:74:4512:18:18:72:540:368:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:allowable amount from federal student loan programs nearly tripled from 18% in 1989-90
3:75:4598:18:18:72:540:350:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to 52% in 1999-2000. At the same time, however, student borrowing from private lending
3:76:4684:18:18:72:540:332:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:institutions increased from negligible amounts in the mid-1990s to $14 billion, almost 20%
3:77:4774:18:18:72:539:314:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f4:B:of all student loans distributed, in the 2004-05 academic year (College Board 2005).Ceteris
3:78:4865:18:18:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f2:B:paribus, this expansion in private lending should have helped alleviate the tight constraints
3:79:4958:18:17:72:351:278:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:imposed by government student loan (GSL) programs.
3:80:5008:17:18:89:539:261:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We study these patterns of college attendance and borrowing in a human capital invest-
3:81:5094:18:18:72:540:243:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ment model that incorporates limited borrowing opportunities from both GSL programs and
3:82:5181:18:18:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:private lenders. Our analysis suggests that all of these trends can be explained by the rising
3:83:5275:18:18:72:540:207:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:returns to schooling (Katz and Autor 1999, Heckman, Lochner and Todd 2008), rising costs
3:84:5363:18:18:72:540:189:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of attendance at U.S. colleges and universities (College Board 2006), and fairly stable real
3:85:5455:18:19:72:540:171:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:borrowing limits associated with GSL programs (Kane 2007) over the last few decades. As
3:86:5542:19:12:85:326:152:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:A:1This has changed in recent years, as we discuss below.
3:87:5597:12:15:85:539:140:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:2Using a variety of empirical approaches and data from the NLSY79, a number of researchers have
3:88:5692:15:12:72:540:125:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:concluded that borrowing constraints had little effect on college-going behavior in the early 1980s. Ellwood
3:89:5800:12:12:72:540:113:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:and Kane (2000) also estimate that family income has become a more important determinant of college-going
3:90:5905:12:0:72:313:101:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:since 1980. See Section 3 for a review of this literature.
4:91:5964:0:18:72:540:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we discuss further below, our model also implies cross-sectional correlations between ability
4:92:6057:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and investment that are more in line with U.S. data than does the traditional model with
4:93:6145:18:18:72:242:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:exogenous borrowing constraints.
4:94:6177:18:18:89:539:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The human capital literature has paid little attention to the nature of borrowing con-
4:95:6263:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:straints. Existing models typically assume that either interest rates increase with the amount
4:96:6357:18:18:72:539:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:borrowed or that there is a fixed maximum amount that individuals can borrow.3Both ap-
4:97:6443:18:18:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:proaches neglect the link between borrowing opportunities and investment decisions that
4:98:6530:18:18:72:540:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:plays a key role in both GSL programs and private lending as we describe below. We show
4:99:6617:18:18:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that without this link, the canonical model of exogenous borrowing constraints predicts
4:100:6704:18:18:72:539:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:anegativerelationship between ability and human capital investment among constrained
4:101:6788:18:18:72:540:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:borrowers when the consumption intertemporal elasticity of substitution (IES) is less than
4:102:6878:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:one. This is troubling, since most empirical estimates of the IES are below one (Browning,
4:103:6968:18:18:72:540:559:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Hansen, and Heckman 1999) and a strong positive ability – college attendance relationship
4:104:7057:18:18:72:540:541:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:exists for all family income (or wealth) levels in both the NLSY79 and NLSY97 (as we show
4:105:7146:18:17:72:540:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in Section 3). Additionally, models with exogenous borrowing constraints offer no insights
4:106:7236:17:18:72:540:506:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:regarding the recent rise in private student lending, the interaction between private and pub-
4:107:7330:18:18:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lic lending, or how lending opportunities respond to important economic and policy changes.
4:108:7421:18:18:72:335:470:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Our framework offers insights on all of these issues.
4:109:7474:18:18:89:539:452:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:GSL programs directly tie student credit to the level of investment — students can
4:110:7556:18:18:72:540:434:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:borrow to help finance college-related expenses only if they are enrolled in school. We show
4:111:7648:18:18:72:540:416:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that private lenders, facing limited repayment incentives from borrowers, will also link credit
4:112:7743:18:18:72:540:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:limits to the level of investment, as well as observable individual characteristics that affect the
4:113:7842:18:18:72:540:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:returns to investment. These features of endogenously determined (or variable) borrowing
4:114:7930:18:18:72:540:362:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:limits help generate a positive relationship between ability and investment (even when the
4:115:8020:18:18:72:540:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:IES is less than one) while still predicting a positive relationship between family resources
4:116:8113:18:18:72:290:326:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:and investment among constrained youth.4
4:117:8153:18:18:89:539:308:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:GSL programs have two distinct forms of limits: (i) a pre-specified maximum loan limit
4:118:8239:18:18:72:540:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(denote this bydmax), and (ii) an endogenous limit that restricts students from borrowing
4:119:8328:18:18:72:540:272:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:more than they spend on their education.5As we show, youth that would like to borrow
4:120:8412:18:17:72:540:254:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:more than they spend on their schooling (i.e. those constrained by the second limit) invest
4:121:8503:17:18:72:540:237:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the same amount in their human capital as if they were completely unconstrained. When
4:122:8588:18:18:72:540:219:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:credit is tied directly to investment, there is no tradeoff between additional investment and
4:123:8681:18:18:72:540:201:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:consumption while in school — every additional dollar of investment can be borrowed (as
4:124:8768:18:16:85:539:183:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:3Studies assuming variable interest rates (or heterogeneous interest rates) include Becker (1975), Cameron
4:125:8874:16:12:72:540:167:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:and Taber (2004) and Card (1995). Studies assuming a fixed limit on borrowing include Belley and Lochner
4:126:8978:12:8:72:534:155:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:(2007), Caucutt and Kumar (2003), Hanushek, Leung, and Yilmaz (2003), and Keane and Wolpin (2001).
4:127:9076:8:16:85:539:147:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:4We refer to these borrowing limits as ‘endogenous’, because they are a function of the borrower’s invest-
4:128:9182:16:12:72:540:131:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:ment behavior. We do not model the determination of these limits in the GSL system; however, borrowing
4:129:9284:12:8:72:498:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:limits set by private lenders are optimally determined from the incentives of borrowers to default.
4:130:9383:8:16:85:539:111:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:5Under the Stafford Loan Program, students face a cumulative loan limit as well as annual borrowing
4:131:9482:16:0:72:366:95:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:limits which increase somewhat with year of post-secondary school.
5:132:9549:0:18:72:539:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:long as investment remains belowdmax). This implies that consumption decisions may be
5:133:9634:18:18:72:539:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:severely distorted even when schooling and investment decisions are not.6Among youth who
5:134:9722:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:would like to invest more than the GSL maximum loan limitdmax, investment is increasing in
5:135:9812:18:18:72:540:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:family income (or wealth) as has been observed for recent student cohorts. Most importantly,
5:136:9904:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:introducing the restriction that borrowing cannot exceed investment substantially reduces
5:137:9993:18:18:72:501:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the set of individuals for which there is a negative ability – investment relationship.
5:138:10080:18:18:89:539:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The recent rise in private lending highlights the importance of studying how private
5:139:10164:18:18:72:540:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lenders determine student credit levels. Even if human capital cannot be directly repossessed
5:140:10257:18:18:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by lenders, creditors can punish defaulting borrowers in a number of ways (e.g. lowering credit
5:141:10352:18:18:72:540:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:scores, seizing assets, garnisheeing a fraction of labor earnings), which tend to have a greater
5:142:10448:18:18:72:540:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:impact on debtors with higher post-school earnings. In our lifecycle model, these mechanisms
5:143:10540:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:effectively link private borrowing limits for students to their abilities and human capital
5:144:10631:18:18:72:539:559:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investments.7Higher ability students who invest more through education will be offered
5:145:10717:18:18:71:539:541:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:more credit by private lenders, because they can credibly commit to re-pay more given the
5:146:10806:18:17:71:539:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:punishments they face upon default. The dependence of credit limits on investment and
5:147:10891:17:18:71:539:506:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f4:B:ability generates a positive ability – investment relationship forall constrained borrowers
5:148:10982:18:18:72:322:488:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:under standard parameterizations of preferences.
5:149:11030:18:18:89:539:470:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our framework incorporates the lending opportunities provided by both GSL programs
5:150:11112:18:18:72:540:452:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and private lenders. This not only provides new insights about human capital investment
5:151:11199:18:18:72:540:434:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:behavior, but it also enables us to better understand the changing role played by private
5:152:11288:18:18:72:540:416:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:financial markets.  In particular, the recent emergence and expansion of private student
5:153:11376:18:18:72:540:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lending can be explained by the stability of government student loan limits over the last few
5:154:11469:18:18:72:540:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:decades and the recent rise in student demand for credit if: (i) GSL maximum borrowing
5:155:11555:18:18:72:540:362:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:limits were high enough to finance unrestricted levels of investment in the early 1980s, (ii)
5:156:11648:18:18:72:540:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:these limits are too low to cover the higher levels of investment desired today, and (iii)
5:157:11738:18:18:72:540:326:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:current students can credibly commit to re-pay more than these limits allow. The evidence
5:158:11827:18:18:72:540:308:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on family income – college attendance patterns in the NLSY79 and NLSY97 is consistent
5:159:11912:18:18:72:540:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with the first two conditions. The higher earnings potential of recent graduates, coupled with
5:160:12006:18:17:72:540:272:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:higher costs of schooling, can explain why more college students are bunching up against
5:161:12094:17:16:85:539:255:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:6Thus, evidence that family resources do not affect educational attainment or financial returns does not
5:162:12198:16:12:72:540:239:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:necessarily imply that credit constraints are non-binding. Standard empirical tests for borrowing constraints
5:163:12307:12:12:72:540:227:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:that rely on differences in educational attainment or marginal rates of return on investment by family
5:164:12409:12:12:72:540:215:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:income (or other categories used to differentiate the ‘constrained’ from ‘unconstrained’) will under-estimate
5:165:12518:12:8:72:533:203:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the fraction of the population that is constrained as well as the adverse impacts of constraints on welfare.
5:166:12626:8:16:85:539:195:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:7Our model of private lending is related to the literature on endogenous credit constraints, which has
5:167:12728:16:12:72:540:179:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:generally focused on implications for risk-sharing and asset prices in endowment economies (e.g. Alvarez
5:168:12832:12:12:72:540:167:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:and Jermann 2000, Fernandez-Villaverde and Krueger 2004, Krueger and Perri 2002, Kehoe and Levine
5:169:12929:12:12:72:540:155:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:1993, and Kocherlakota 1996) or firm dynamics (e.g. Albuquerque and Hopenhayn 2004, Monge-Naranjo
5:170:13026:12:12:72:540:143:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:2008). Our assumed punishments for default are similar to those employed by Livshits, MacGee, and Tertilt
5:171:13131:12:12:72:540:131:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:(2007) in their analysis of bankruptcy over the lifecycle. Andolfatto and Gervais (2006) study human capital
5:172:13239:12:12:72:540:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:accumulation with limited commitment, but they focus on the optimal set of intergenerational transfers and
5:173:13345:12:0:72:290:107:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:not on cross-sectional implications for investment.
6:174:13397:0:18:72:540:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:GSL maximum borrowing limits (Berkner 2000 and Titus 2002). This creates new demand
6:175:13480:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for private lenders to step in, offering more credit to those who can credibly commit to
6:176:13568:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:repay. With rising returns to schooling, commitments to repay become credible for more
6:177:13654:18:18:72:540:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and more college students, suggesting that condition (iii) is likely to be met. Our lifecycle
6:178:13747:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model calibrated to U.S. data generates these patterns in response to a rise in both the
6:179:13835:18:18:72:214:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:returns and costs of college.
6:180:13864:18:18:89:539:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The rest of the paper proceeds as follows. In the next section, we describe borrowing
6:181:13949:18:18:72:540:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:opportunities from U.S. GSL programs and the recent emergence of private lenders.  In
6:182:14034:18:18:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the third section, we discuss evidence on the relationship between ability, family income
6:183:14123:18:18:72:540:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and college attendance in the U.S. and briefly survey the literature on the prevalence of
6:184:14212:18:18:72:540:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:credit constraints. In Section 4, we develop a simple two-period human capital investment
6:185:14301:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model to analytically compare the cross-sectional implications for borrowing and investment
6:186:14392:18:18:72:540:559:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:under alternative assumptions about credit markets. Section 5 extends our framework to
6:187:14478:18:18:72:540:541:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a lifecycle model that incorporates government subsidies for education. Qualitative results
6:188:14569:18:17:72:540:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:derived for the two-period model carry over to this environment. We calibrate this model
6:189:14657:17:18:72:540:506:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:using U.S. data on schooling, ability, government subsidies, and post-school earnings. This
6:190:14748:18:18:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:quantitative analysis shows that our model with both public and private lending does a good
6:191:14839:18:18:72:540:470:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:job reproducing observed cross-sectional human capital investment patterns for the early
6:192:14927:18:18:72:540:452:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1980s and 2000s. The model with exogenous constraints does not. Our model of endogenous
6:193:15014:18:18:72:540:434:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:constraints also explains both the increased effects of family income on college-going and the
6:194:15108:18:18:72:540:416:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:rising importance of private lending for recent cohorts as optimal responses to the increased
6:195:15201:18:40:72:482:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:costs of and financial returns to human capital investment. Section 6 concludes.
6:196:15281:40:30:72:333:358:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:2  Available Sources of Credit
6:197:15311:30:18:72:540:328:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This section briefly reviews the primary sources of borrowing used for human capital invest-
6:198:15403:18:18:72:540:310:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ment in the U.S. We first describe key institutional features of GSL programs, which we
6:199:15490:18:18:72:540:292:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:incorporate in our endogenous constraint models below. Then, we discuss the rise of private
6:200:15581:18:34:72:261:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lending for post-secondary schooling.
6:201:15618:34:26:72:370:240:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:2.1  Government Student Loan Programs
6:202:15655:26:18:72:540:214:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Federal student loans are an important source of finance for higher education in the U.S., ac-
6:203:15749:18:18:72:540:196:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:counting for 71% of the federal student aid disbursed in 2003-04.8Most of these government-
6:204:15840:18:18:72:540:178:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:backed loans are provided through the Stafford Loan program, which awarded nearly $50
6:205:15925:18:18:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:billion to students in the 2003-04 academic year, compared to the disbursement of $1.6 billion
6:206:16019:18:17:72:540:142:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:through the Perkins Loan program. Slightly more than $7 billion was awarded to parents of
6:207:16108:17:0:85:433:125:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:A:8Many other countries have similar types of government student loan programs.
7:208:16186:0:18:72:537:775:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:undergraduate students in the form of Parent Loans for Undergraduate Students (PLUS).9
7:209:16272:18:18:89:539:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:GSL programs generally have three important features. First, lending is directly tied to
7:210:16360:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investment. Students (or parents) can only borrow up to the total cost of college (including
7:211:16452:18:18:72:540:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tuition, room, board, books, supplies, transportation, computers, and other expenses directly
7:212:16545:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:related to schooling) less any other financial aid they receive in the forms of grants or
7:213:16634:18:18:72:540:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:scholarships. Thus, students cannot borrow from GSL programs to finance non-schooling
7:214:16719:18:18:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:related consumption goods or activities. Second, student loan programs set fixed upper limits
7:215:16812:18:18:72:540:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on the total amount of credit available for each student. Students face both cumulative and
7:216:16903:18:18:72:539:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:annual loan limits for U.S. federal loan programs.10Third, loans covered by GSL programs
7:217:16991:18:18:72:481:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:typically have extended enforcement rules compared to unsecured private loans.
7:218:17069:18:18:89:539:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Historically, private lenders have provided the capital to student borrowers (and their
7:219:17156:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:parents) under the Stafford and PLUS programs, the government guaranteeing those loans
7:220:17242:18:18:72:540:559:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with a promise to cover any unpaid amounts. Since the 1994-95 academic year, the federal
7:221:17330:18:18:72:540:541:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:government has begun to directly provide these loans to some students under the same rules
7:222:17420:18:17:72:539:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and terms.11While Stafford loans are disbursed to students, PLUS loans can be taken out by
7:223:17510:17:18:72:540:506:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:parents to help cover the costs of their children’s schooling. Another major federal student
7:224:17602:18:18:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:loan program, the Perkins Loan Program, provides an additional source of government funds
7:225:17691:18:18:72:540:470:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to students most in need; however, its loan offerings depend on the level of program funding
7:226:17783:18:18:72:540:452:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:at the post-secondary institution attended by a student. In practice, Perkins loans make up
7:227:17874:18:18:72:350:434:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a small fraction of federal student loan disbursements.
7:228:17929:18:18:89:539:416:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Table 1 reports loan limits (based on the dependency status and class level of each
7:229:18012:18:18:72:540:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:student) for Stafford and Perkins student loan programs for the period 1993-2007.12In recent
7:230:18104:18:18:72:540:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:years, dependent students could borrow up to $23,000 from the Stafford Loan Program over
7:231:18192:18:18:72:540:362:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the course of their undergraduate careers. Independent students could borrow roughly twice
7:232:18282:18:18:72:540:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that amount, although most traditional undergraduates would not fall into this category.
7:233:18370:18:18:72:540:326:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Qualified undergraduates from low income families could receive as much as $20,000 in
7:234:18455:18:18:72:540:308:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Perkins loans, depending on their need and post-secondary institution. It is important to
7:235:18544:18:18:72:540:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:note, however, that amounts offered through this program have typically been less than
7:236:18630:18:18:72:539:272:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:mandated limits.13Student borrowers can defer loan re-payments until six (Stafford) to nine
7:237:18721:18:12:85:537:254:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:9See The College Board (2006) for details about financial aid disbursements and their trends over time.
7:238:18824:12:16:81:539:242:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:10Since 1993-94, the PLUS loan program no longer has a fixed maximum borrowing limit; however, parents
7:239:18926:16:8:72:516:226:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:still cannot borrow more than the total cost of college less other financial aid received by the student.
7:240:19031:8:16:81:539:218:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:11The Stafford program offers both subsidized and unsubsidized loans. The government covers the interest
7:241:19135:16:12:72:540:202:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:on subsidized loans while students are enrolled. Unsubsidized loans accrue interest over this period; however,
7:242:19245:12:11:72:540:190:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the student is not required to make any payments until after leaving school. To qualify for subsidized loans,
7:243:19354:11:12:72:540:179:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:students must demonstrate financial need on the basis of family income, dependency status, and the cost of
7:244:19460:12:12:72:540:167:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the school attended. Most students under age 24 are considered dependent, and their parents’ income is an
7:245:19565:12:12:72:540:155:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:important determinant of their financial need. Prior to the introduction of unsubsidized Stafford Loans in
7:246:19671:12:12:72:540:143:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the early 1990s, Supplemental Loans to Students (SLS) were an alternative source of unsubsidized federal
7:247:19775:12:9:72:208:131:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:loans for independent students.
7:248:19806:9:12:81:531:122:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:?:12Stafford loan limits for freshman, sophomores, and graduate students increased slightly in July, 2007.
7:249:19910:12:0:81:539:110:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:13Parents that do not have an adverse credit rating can borrow up to the cost of schooling from the PLUS

8:273:20987:18:18:89:539:298:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 1 shows how annual Stafford loan limits for dependent undergraduate students
8:274:21070:18:18:72:539:280:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:have evolved from 1980–81 to 2006–07 (denominated in year 2000 dollars).14In most years,
8:275:21158:18:17:72:540:262:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the cumulative loan limit is equal to or slightly greater than the sum of all five annual loan

8:277:21338:18:18:72:540:227:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:otherwise, inflation has continuously eroded these limits. The entry year into college has
8:278:21428:18:18:72:540:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:seen the greatest erosion in real borrowing opportunities — a 44% decline from 1982–83 to

8:280:21605:22:12:72:540:169:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:program, with repayment typically beginning within 60 days of loan disbursement.  Dependent students
8:281:21705:12:12:72:540:157:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:whose parents do not qualify for PLUS loans (due to a bad credit rating) are able to borrow up to the

9:305:22283:237:18:72:540:469:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:period. By contrast, third- through fifth-year undergraduates were able to borrow nearly
9:306:22371:18:18:72:540:451:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:20% more in 2002–03 than in 1982–83 due to more substantial nominal increases in 1986–87
9:307:22459:18:18:72:540:433:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and 1993–94. Cumulative Stafford loan limits were almost identical in real terms in 1982–83
9:308:22550:18:17:72:146:415:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:and 2002–03.16
9:309:22564:17:18:89:539:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Student loans covered by these federal programs have extended enforcement rules com-
9:310:22648:18:18:72:540:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:pared to typical unsecured private loans. Except in very special circumstances, these loans
9:311:22739:18:18:72:540:362:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cannot generally be expunged through bankruptcy.  If a suitable re-payment plan is not
9:312:22825:18:18:72:540:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:agreed upon with the lender once a borrower enters default, the default status will be re-
9:313:22915:18:18:72:540:326:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ported to credit bureaus and collection costs (up to 25% of the balance due) may be added
9:314:23004:18:18:72:539:308:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to the amount outstanding.17Up to 15% of the borrower’s wages can also be garnisheed.
9:315:23089:18:18:72:540:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Moreover, federal tax refunds can be seized and applied toward any outstanding balance.
9:316:23176:18:18:72:540:272:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Other sanctions include a possible hold on college transcripts, ineligibility for further federal
9:317:23273:18:18:72:432:254:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:student loans, and ineligibility for future deferments or forbearances.18
9:318:23346:18:15:81:539:236:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:16Throughout most of this period, loan limits for independent undergraduates remained about twice the
9:319:23447:15:12:72:540:221:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:amounts available to dependent students. Stafford loan limits for graduate students declined by about 35%
9:320:23552:12:12:72:540:209:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:in real terms from 1986–87 to 2006–07, roughly the time our NLSY respondents would have began attending
9:321:23655:12:9:72:143:197:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:graduate school.
9:322:23671:9:12:81:454:188:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:?:17Formally, a borrower is considered to be in default once a payment is 270 days late.
9:323:23757:12:15:81:539:176:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:18Since the early 1990s, the government has also begun to punish educational institutions with high student
9:324:23864:15:0:72:465:161:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:default rates by making their students ineligible to borrow from federal lending programs.
10:325:23955:0:26:72:354:775:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:2.2  The Emergence of Private Lenders
10:326:23992:26:18:72:540:749:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Until the mid-1990s, few private lenders offered loans to students outside the GSL programs.
10:327:24084:18:18:72:540:731:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In 1995-96, total non-federal student loans amounted to $1.3 billion.  By 2004-05, that
10:328:24171:18:18:72:540:713:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:amount had risen to almost $14 billion (nearly 20% of all student loans distributed).19Private
10:329:24265:18:18:72:540:695:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:student loans generally charge higher interest rates than Stafford or Perkins loans and are,
10:330:24357:18:18:72:540:677:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:therefore, typically taken after exhausting available credit from GSL programs. Thus, the
10:331:24446:18:18:72:540:659:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:rise in borrowing from private lenders outside the Stafford and Perkins Loan Programs
10:332:24531:18:18:72:540:641:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:suggests that the GSL limits are no longer enough to satisfy many students’ demands for
10:333:24618:18:18:72:540:623:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:credit. Private loans are most prevalent among graduate students (especially in professional
10:334:24710:18:18:72:540:605:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:schools) and undergraduates at high-cost private universities (Wegmann, Cunningham and
10:335:24796:18:17:72:154:587:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Merisotis 2003).
10:336:24812:17:18:89:539:570:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:While many private student lending programs are loosely structured like the federal GSL
10:337:24899:18:18:72:540:552:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:programs (i.e. many limit borrowing to the cost of schooling less financial aid or a fixed upper
10:338:24995:18:18:72:540:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:limit on total borrowing), they vary substantially in their terms and eligibility requirements.
10:339:25090:18:18:72:540:516:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Private lending programs typically use a broader concept of schooling costs than do GSL
10:340:25177:18:18:72:540:498:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:programs, often allowing students to borrow against previous educational expenses or ex-
10:341:25265:18:18:72:540:480:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:penses for study abroad. Specified maximum loan limits are generally quite high, especially
10:342:25356:18:18:72:540:462:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for students in professional schools (e.g. law, medical, or business schools); however, actual
10:343:25450:18:18:72:540:444:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:amounts offered to students vary depending on their creditworthiness, institution attended,
10:344:25541:18:18:72:540:426:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and area of study. A cosigner with a good credit history tends to improve the terms of any
10:345:25631:18:40:72:395:408:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:loans and can affect whether a loan is offered in the first place.
10:346:25697:40:30:72:538:368:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:F:3  Evidence on the Role of Ability and Family Income
10:347:25749:30:18:72:540:338:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we discuss the empirical relationship between family income, cognitive ability
10:348:25844:18:17:72:540:320:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and college attendance.  We review the recent literature and offer some new evidence in
10:349:25931:17:18:72:540:303:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:documenting three stylized facts on investment in human capital. First, there was a weak
10:350:26019:18:18:72:540:285:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:link between family income and college attendance in the early 1980s. Second, for very recent
10:351:26112:18:18:72:540:267:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:student cohorts, there is a much stronger relationship between family income (or wealth) and
10:352:26204:18:18:72:540:249:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:college attendance. Third, in both the early 1980s and the early 2000s, there is a strong
10:353:26293:18:18:72:540:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:positive relationship between college attendance and cognitive ability or achievement (as
10:354:26382:18:18:72:421:213:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:measured by scores on the Armed Forces Qualifying Test, AFQT).20
10:355:26446:18:18:89:539:195:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Many empirical studies using NLSY79 data conclude that borrowing constraints played
10:356:26529:18:18:72:540:177:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:little role in college attendance decisions in the U.S. during the early 1980s.  Cameron
10:357:26617:18:15:81:539:159:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:19These figures do not include student borrowing on credit cards, which has also increased considerably
10:358:26720:15:9:72:260:144:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:over this period. See College Board (2005).
10:359:26763:9:15:81:539:135:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:20This evidence is largely based on the NLSY79 and NLSY97. AFQT scores are widely used as measures
10:360:26861:15:12:72:540:120:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:of cognitive achievement by social scientists and are strongly correlated with post-school earnings conditional
10:361:26972:12:0:72:499:108:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:on educational attainment. See, e.g., Cawley,et al.2000. Appendix A provides additional details.
11:362:27069:0:18:72:540:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and Heckman (1998, 1999) find that after controlling for family background, AFQT scores,
11:363:27157:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and unobserved heterogeneity, family income has little effect on college enrollment rates.
11:364:27247:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Carneiro and Heckman (2002) also estimate small differences in college enrollment rates
11:365:27334:18:18:72:540:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and other college-going outcomes by family income after accounting for differences in family
11:366:27426:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:background and AFQT. Cameron and Taber (2004) find little evidence of differential returns
11:367:27516:18:18:72:540:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to school that would be consistent with borrowing constraints. Keane and Wolpin (2001)
11:368:27602:18:18:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:estimate a structural model of schooling and work that incorporates constraints on borrowing
11:369:27694:18:18:72:540:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and parental transfers that may depend on child schooling decisions. While they estimate
11:370:27782:18:18:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:very tight borrowing limits (much more stringent than federal student loan limits), they find
11:371:27875:18:18:72:397:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:little effect of borrowing constraints on educational attainment.
11:372:27940:18:18:89:539:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Much has changed since the early 1980s, when the NLSY79 respondents made their
11:373:28018:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:college attendance decisions. Financial returns to schooling have risen dramatically (Katz
11:374:28108:18:18:72:540:559:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and Autor 1999, Heckman, Lochner, and Todd 2008) as have the costs of tuition, fees, room,
11:375:28198:18:18:72:540:541:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and board at U.S. colleges and universities (College Board 2005). At the same time, real
11:376:28286:18:17:72:540:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:borrowing limits associated with government student loan programs have remained stable
11:377:28372:17:18:72:206:506:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:or declined (see Figure 1).
11:378:28399:18:18:89:539:488:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:These trends appear to have increased the importance of borrowing constraints. Indeed,
11:379:28485:18:18:72:540:470:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the fraction of all undergraduate borrowers that borrowed the maximum limit from the
11:380:28569:18:18:72:540:452:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:federal Stafford Student Loan Program went up from only 18% in 1989-90 to 52% in 1999-
11:381:28655:18:18:72:540:434:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:2000. Among dependent undergraduates, the fraction increases to nearly 70% of all borrowers
11:382:28746:18:18:72:540:416:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in 1999-2000 (Berkner 2000 and Titus 2002). Moreover, Belley and Lochner (2007) show
11:383:28830:18:18:72:540:398:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that family income has become a much more important determinant of college attendance
11:384:28915:18:18:72:539:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for college-going decisions in the early 2000s.21Youth from high income families in the
11:385:29002:18:18:72:540:362:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:NLSY97 are sixteen percentage points more likely to attend college than are youth from
11:386:29088:18:18:72:540:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:low income families, conditional on AFQT scores, family composition, parental age and
11:387:29173:18:18:72:540:326:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:education, race/ethnicity, and urban/rural residence. This is nearly twice the effect observed
11:388:29267:18:18:72:540:308:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the NLSY79. The combined effects of family income and wealth are even more dramatic
11:389:29353:18:18:72:540:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the NLSY97. Comparing youth from the highest family income and wealth quartiles to
11:390:29438:18:18:72:540:272:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:those from the lowest quartiles yields an estimated difference in college attendance rates of
11:391:29531:18:17:72:478:254:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:nearly 30 percentage points after controlling for ability and family background.
11:392:29611:17:18:89:539:237:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Next, we examine the effects of ability (as measured by AFQT scores) on college atten-
11:393:29697:18:18:72:540:219:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dance for youth from different family income (or wealth) backgrounds in both the NLSY79
11:394:29784:18:18:72:540:201:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and NLSY97. (See Appendix A for a detailed description of the data and variables used
11:395:29869:18:16:81:539:183:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:21Ellwood and Kane (2000) argue that college attendance differences by family income were already be-
11:396:29970:16:12:72:540:167:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:coming more important by the early 1990s. Using data on youth of college-ages in the 1970s, 1980s, and
11:397:30072:12:12:72:540:155:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:1990s (from the Health and Retirement Survey), Brown, Seshadri, and Scholz (2007) estimate that borrowing
11:398:30177:12:12:72:540:143:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:constraints limit college-going; however, they do not examine whether constraints have become more limit-
11:399:30282:12:12:72:540:131:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:ing in recent years. While Stinebrickner and Stinebrickner (2007) find little effect of borrowing constraints
11:400:30391:12:12:72:540:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:(defined by the self-reported desire to borrow more for school) on overall college dropout rates for a recent
11:401:30500:12:12:72:540:107:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:cohort of students at Berea College, they find substantial differences in dropout rates between those who are
11:402:30609:12:0:72:535:95:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:constrained and those who are not. They do not study the effects of borrowing constraints on attendance.
12:403:30714:0:18:72:540:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:here.) Figure 2 shows college attendance rates by AFQT quartiles and either family income
12:404:30803:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:or family wealth quartiles in the NLSY79 and NLSY97. For all family income or wealth cat-
12:405:30892:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:egories in both NLSY samples, we observe substantial increases in college attendance with
12:406:30981:18:18:72:540:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:AFQT. The difference in attendance rates between the highest and lowest ability quartiles
12:407:31070:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:range from .47 to .68 depending on the family income or wealth quartile. Most importantly
12:408:31159:18:18:72:540:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for our theoretical analysis below, there is no indication that the effects of ability are negative
12:409:31258:18:18:72:526:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for lower income youth who are most likely to be constrained, especially in the NLSY97.
12:410:31345:18:18:89:539:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Of course, AFQT scores may be correlated with other family background variables that
12:411:31429:18:18:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:influence college attendance decisions conditional on family resources. We, therefore, control
12:412:31523:18:18:72:540:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for a host of other family background measures in addition to AFQT quartiles using ordinary
12:413:31614:18:18:72:540:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:least squares. Table 2 reports the estimated effects of AFQT (these estimates reflect the
12:414:31703:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:difference in attendance rates between the reported AFQT quartile and AFQT quartile 1)
12:415:31789:18:18:72:539:559:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on college attendance after controlling for family background characteristics.22Results are
12:416:31880:18:18:72:540:541:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:reported for separate regressions by family income or wealth quartile. The estimates confirm
12:417:31972:18:17:72:540:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the general patterns observed in Figure 2:  ability has strong positive effects on college
12:418:32062:17:40:72:469:506:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:attendance for all family income and wealth quartiles in both NLSY samples.
12:419:32137:40:30:72:434:466:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:4  Basic Models of Borrowing Constraints
12:420:32177:30:18:72:540:436:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We now develop a simple two-period model to study the impact of credit constraints on
12:421:32262:18:18:72:540:418:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investment in human capital. We allow for some generality in preferences and skill production
12:422:32355:18:18:72:540:400:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and derive the qualitative investment – wealth and investment – ability relationships implied
12:423:32448:18:18:72:540:382:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by alternative forms of credit constraints. We show that these relationships depend crucially
12:424:32541:18:18:72:540:364:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on the nature of credit constraints and evaluate the empirical relevance of the different
12:425:32630:18:35:72:358:346:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:models based on the empirical findings discussed above.
12:426:32685:35:25:72:186:311:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.1  The Model
12:427:32699:25:18:72:540:286:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Consider two-period-lived individuals who invest in schooling in the first period and work in
12:428:32792:18:18:72:212:268:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the second. Preferences are

12:430:32839:24:24:72:533:226:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:wherectis consumption in periodst∈{0,1},β >0 is a discount factor andu(·) satisfies:
12:431:32923:24:18:72:540:202:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f4:F:Assumption 1.u:R+→Ris strictly increasing, strictly concave, twice continuously
12:432:33002:18:24:72:267:184:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:differentiable andlimc↘0u′(c) = +∞.
12:433:33037:24:18:89:539:160:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Each individual is endowed with initial financial assetsw≥0 and abilitya >0. Initial
12:434:33121:18:18:72:540:142:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:assets represent all transfers from parents and other family members. Ability represents all
12:435:33213:18:16:81:539:124:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:22We control for the following: gender, race/ethnicity, mother’s education, intact family during adoles-

16:555:38043:25:18:72:540:594:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:At least since Becker (1975), economists have introduced financial market imperfections in

17:623:40431:18:18:89:539:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:For unconstrained individuals, the trade-off between early and late consumption is de-
17:624:40517:18:18:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:termined by the return on financial assets, and investment equalshU(a). Constrained indi-

17:626:40699:18:18:72:540:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Their trade-off between early and late consumption is given by the rate of return on human

17:647:41091:29:18:72:540:482:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:equating the marginal cost of investment in terms of early utility with the marginal benefit
17:648:41183:18:18:72:411:464:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in terms of late utility when borrowing is set to the maximum,d0.
17:649:41248:18:18:89:539:446:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We highlight three important results. First, constrained investment never exceeds un-
17:650:41333:18:18:72:540:428:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:constrained investment, i.e.hX(a, w)≤hU(a). This result holds for all forms of constraints
17:651:41423:18:18:72:540:410:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:considered in the paper.  Second, constrained investment is strictly increasing in wealth.
17:652:41513:18:18:72:540:392:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Third, constrained investment may be increasing or decreasing in ability depending on the
17:653:41602:18:31:72:395:374:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:intertemporal elasticity of substitution (IES),−u′(c)/[cu′′(c)].
17:654:41666:31:18:72:540:343:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f4:F:Proposition 1LethX(a, w)andhU(a)denote, respectively, the optimal investment with
17:655:41747:18:18:71:539:325:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:F:and without the constraint (EXC).  If (EXC) binds, then:  (i)hX(a, w)< hU(a); (ii)
17:656:41829:18:18:71:539:307:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f4:F:hX(a, w)is strictly increasing inw; (iii) the marginal return on human capital investment,
17:657:41919:18:0:71:87:289:12:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f8:C:af′

17:662:42009:18:30:72:336:271:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:thenhX(a, w)isstrictly decreasingin ability,a.
17:663:42055:30:18:89:539:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Results (i), (ii) and (iii) are well-known and already discussed in Becker (1975). They
17:664:42142:18:18:72:540:223:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are central to the empirical literature on credit constraints.  For instance, Cameron and
17:665:42231:18:18:72:540:205:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Heckman (1998, 1999), Ellwood and Kane (2000), Carneiro and Heckman (2002), and Belley
17:666:42317:18:18:72:540:187:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and Lochner (2007) empirically examine if youth from lower income families acquire less
17:667:42404:18:18:72:540:169:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:schooling, conditional on family background and ability.  Lang (1993), Card (1995), and
17:668:42491:18:18:72:540:151:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Cameron and Taber (2004) explore the prediction that the marginal return on human capital
17:669:42580:18:18:72:325:133:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investment exceeds the return on financial assets.
17:670:42630:18:18:89:539:115:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The more interesting and novel result is (iv). It reveals a serious shortcoming of this
17:671:42717:18:0:72:540:97:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model that the literature has not recognized. The model predicts anegativerelationship
18:672:42805:0:18:72:539:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:between ability and investment for an IES below one.24This is a serious problem, because
18:673:42893:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:most estimates of the IES are less than one (see Browning, Hansen, Heckman 1999) and a
18:674:42979:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:positive relationship between ability and investment is a robust empirical regularity. The
18:675:43069:18:18:72:540:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:relationship between ability and investment for constrained youth derives from two opposing
18:676:43160:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:effects. On the one hand, an increase in ability raises the financial returns to investment,
18:677:43252:18:18:72:540:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:which encourages investment.  On the other hand, ability raises lifetime income, which
18:678:43338:18:18:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:encourages early consumption. Since constrained youth can only increase early consumption
18:679:43427:18:18:72:540:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by investing less, strong preferences for smoothing (i.e. IES≤1) imply that the second effect
18:680:43520:18:35:72:127:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dominates.
18:681:43530:35:25:72:419:596:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.4  Government Student Loan (GSL) Programs
18:682:43573:25:18:72:540:571:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Consider now, credit limits that exhibit the key features of GSL programs. First, lending is
18:683:43665:18:18:72:540:553:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f2:B:tied to investmentand cannot be used to finance non-schooling related consumption goods
18:684:43752:18:18:72:135:535:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:or activities:
18:685:43766:18:24:289:539:517:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:A:d≤h.(TIC)
18:686:43775:24:18:72:540:493:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In the absence of other sources of credit, (TIC) is equivalent toc0≥w. This constraint is
18:687:43864:18:18:72:540:475:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:endogenous in the sense that borrowing limits depend on the amount of investment under-
18:688:43951:18:18:72:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f11:B:taken by an individual. Second, borrowing is constrained by an upper limit 0< dmax<∞
18:689:44035:18:18:72:253:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for the total credit to each student:
18:690:44072:18:25:282:540:421:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:A:d≤dmax.(7)
18:691:44082:25:18:72:540:396:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This second constraint is effectively the same as the exogenous constraint above. The overall
18:692:44175:18:29:72:295:378:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:credit limits induced by GSL programs are:
18:693:44217:29:30:258:540:349:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:A:d≤min{h, dmax}.(GSLC)
18:694:44238:30:18:72:435:319:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We use the superscriptGto refer to allocations under a GSL program.
18:695:44305:18:18:89:539:301:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To see the implications of (TIC), first assume that it is the only constraint.25In this
18:696:44392:18:18:72:540:283:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:case, individuals are unconstrained if their desired investment exceeds desired borrowing, i.e.
18:697:44487:18:18:72:540:265:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:B:hU(a)≥dU(a, w). These are individuals who hold wealth that exceeds the finite threshold
18:698:44574:18:18:74:539:247:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B: ̃wmin(a), which is increasing in abilityabut at a slower rate thanwXmin(a), as shown in
18:699:44662:18:18:72:540:229:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Appendix B. In comparison to exogenous constraint models, (TIC) is more (less) stringent
18:700:44750:18:18:72:539:211:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f7:B:than (EXC) for low (high) ability individuals in the sense that  ̃wmin(a)> wXmin(a) ifhU(a)<
18:701:44842:18:18:72:278:193:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:B:d0, and  ̃wmin(a)< wXmin(a) ifhU(a)> d0.
18:702:44882:18:16:81:539:175:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:24AnIES≤1 is only a sufficient condition for a negative ability – investment relationship. More generally,
18:703:44988:16:12:72:540:159:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the model may predict a negative relationship for IES values greater than one. While the model formally
18:704:45091:12:12:72:540:147:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:abstracts from foregone earnings, it is isomorphic to one in which foregone earnings for any given investment,
18:705:45201:12:12:72:539:135:10:g_d0_f20:10:g_d0_f6:F:h, are independent of ability. Result (iv) holds more generally in a model with foregone earnings as long as
18:706:45309:12:8:72:307:123:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:youth wage rates are not strictly decreasing in ability.
18:707:45365:8:16:81:539:115:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:25This is the most appropriate model when upper borrowing limits are non-existent or set very high (e.g.
18:708:45469:16:0:72:237:99:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:PLUS program for students’ parents).
19:709:45507:0:18:89:539:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:If (TIC) is the only binding condition, thend=handc0=w. As such, the maximization
19:710:45588:18:18:72:160:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:problem becomes

19:712:45606:8:0:231:236:731:8:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f10:?:h
19:713:45607:0:23:246:388:739:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f2:?:{u(w) +βu[af(h)−Rh]},
19:714:45628:23:18:72:539:716:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:which is equivalent to maximizing late consumption,c1=af(h)−Rh. In this case, optimal
19:715:45713:18:18:72:337:698:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investment equals the unconstrained amounthU(a).
19:716:45761:18:18:89:539:680:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Tying borrowing to investment removes the conflict between smoothing consumption and
19:717:45845:18:18:72:540:662:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:maximizing net lifetime resources. If (TIC) is the only constraint on credit, everyone ends up
19:718:45939:18:18:72:539:644:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investing the unconstrained amount,hU(a), regardless of initial wealth. Only consumption
19:719:46027:18:18:72:540:626:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:decisions are distorted by the constraint. Empirical tests based on investment differences
19:720:46117:18:18:72:540:608:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by family resources would always conclude that borrowing constraints are non-binding, even
19:721:46207:18:18:72:540:590:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f4:B:when consumption allocations are distorted.Empirical tests must use measures of consump-
19:722:46295:18:18:72:273:572:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:tion over time to detect this constraint.
19:723:46336:18:18:89:539:554:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Consider now the full GSL constraint (GSLC).  For ease of exposition, assume that

19:728:46765:18:18:89:539:465:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We now examine the relationship between investment, ability and wealth under a GSL

20:776:50185:18:18:89:539:362:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Three points about investment under the GSL are worth highlighting.  First, invest-

22:810:51910:0:18:72:540:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Altogether, the implied investment – ability and investment – wealth relationships in the
22:811:51999:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:GSL model are closer to the empirical findings discussed earlier. In particular, the set of in-
22:812:52094:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dividuals whose investment declines with ability is smaller than in the traditional exogenous
22:813:52187:18:18:72:161:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:constraint model.
22:814:52204:18:18:89:539:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Whendmax=d0, credit is more limited under the GSL than under the exogenous con-
22:815:52283:18:18:71:539:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:straint, because the GSL imposes an additional restriction on borrowing. While the extra
22:816:52371:18:18:71:539:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:restriction (TIC) adversely affects utility and early consumption levels, it encourages invest-
22:817:52466:18:18:71:539:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ment relative to the exogenous constraint model. The following proposition compares the
22:818:52553:18:29:71:526:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:allocations and utility under the GSL, exogenous constraints, and unconstrained models:
22:819:52640:29:18:71:539:602:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f4:F:Lemma 2Imposed0=dmaxand let{hm, cm0, cm1, Um}, denote the optimal allocations and
22:820:52721:18:28:72:478:584:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:attained utilities in modelsm=U, X, Gfor arbitrarily fixed(a, w)∈R2+. Then:
22:821:52796:28:28:113:498:556:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:C:hU≥hG≥hX,cU0≥cX0≥cG0,cG1≥cU1≥cX1,UU≥UX≥UG,
22:822:52838:28:29:72:540:528:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:F:and any of the inequalities is strict if the extra constraint between a pair of models is binding.
22:823:52936:29:18:89:539:499:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:On one hand, the addition of (TIC) reduces the feasible set and hence attainable utility
22:824:53024:18:18:72:540:481:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:levels. On the other hand, it relieves the tension between income maximization and con-
22:825:53111:18:18:72:540:463:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sumption smoothing that is inherent in the exogenous constraint model. This is particularly
22:826:53202:18:18:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:important for those who would like to borrow more than they want to invest. The GSL
22:827:53285:18:18:72:540:427:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:forces these individuals to invest more than they would otherwise choose and can dramati-
22:828:53374:18:34:72:274:409:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cally distort their consumption profiles.
22:829:53415:34:26:72:427:375:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.5  Private Lending under Limited Commitment
22:830:53460:26:18:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The inalienability of human capital and the lack of other forms of collateral are standard jus-
22:831:53555:18:18:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tifications for introducing borrowing constraints to models of human capital accumulation.
22:832:53645:18:17:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Most often, however, the nature of credit limits that arise from these incentive problems
22:833:53734:17:18:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is left unexplored. In this subsection, we consider constraints that arise from the limited
22:834:53825:18:18:72:540:278:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:commitment of borrowers to repay loans. In the following subsection, we consider the coex-
22:835:53915:18:18:72:540:260:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:istence of private lenders that face limited commitment from borrowers with a GSL program
22:836:54004:18:18:72:203:242:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that is perfectly enforced.
22:837:54031:18:18:89:539:224:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:A rational borrower repays his loans if and only if the cost of repaying is lower than
22:838:54117:18:18:72:540:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the cost of defaulting. The incentive to repay can be foreseen by rational lenders who, in
22:839:54207:18:18:72:540:188:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:response, limit their supply of credit.27Since penalties for default impose a larger monetary
22:840:54300:18:18:72:540:170:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cost for borrowers with higher earnings and assets — only so much can be taken from someone
22:841:54391:18:18:72:540:152:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with little to take — the credit offered to an individual is directly related to his perceived
22:842:54485:18:18:72:540:134:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:future earnings. Since earnings are determined by ability and investment, credit limits and
22:843:54576:18:17:72:347:116:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:investments will be jointly determined in equilibrium.
22:844:54630:17:0:81:500:99:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:?:27Gropp, Scholz, and White (1997) empirically support this form of response by private lenders.
23:845:54727:0:18:89:539:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To examine human capital in this environment, we first characterize the credit con-
23:846:54810:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:straints that arise endogenously from limited commitment. To this end, we simply assume
23:847:54897:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that lenders can punish defaulting borrowers by garnisheeing a fraction  ̃κ∈(0,1) of their
23:848:54987:18:18:72:539:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:earnings.28In Section 5.5, we incorporate additional punishments for default in a richer
23:849:55075:18:18:72:150:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lifecycle model.
23:850:55091:18:18:89:539:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Given the punishment for default, repayment decisions are simple: borrowers repay (prin-
23:851:55179:18:18:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:cipal plus interest on a debtd) if the paymentRdis less than the punishment cost  ̃κaf(h).
23:852:55269:18:25:72:343:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Foreseeing this, lenders choose to limit borrowing to:
23:853:55323:25:25:273:540:624:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:A:d≤κaf(h) ,(8)
23:854:55336:25:18:72:445:599:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whereκ≡R−1 ̃κ < R−1. We use the superscriptLto refer to this model.
23:855:55403:18:18:89:539:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Individuals are unconstrained if their desired borrowing is compatible with its credible
23:856:55491:18:0:72:229:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f7:B:repayment, i.e.dU(a, w)≤κaf

23:904:57359:18:17:72:203:128:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f4:C:IES(c0)≥1−(1 +R)κ.
23:905:57377:17:16:81:539:111:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:28Penalty avoidance actions like re-locating, working in the informal economy, borrowing from loan sharks,

24:907:57585:0:18:89:539:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As noted earlier, the responsiveness of credit limits to ability and investment creates
24:908:57672:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a tendency for more able persons to be unconstrained. Proposition 3 shows that this re-
24:909:57759:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sponsiveness also creates a tendency for constrained investmenthL(a, w) to be increasing in
24:910:57850:18:18:72:539:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ability. In Section 5, we derive the implied values ofκwithin a more empirically plausible
24:911:57940:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:environment and find that foranyreasonable values of the IES, this model produces a pos-
24:912:58028:18:18:72:540:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:itive ability – investment relationship. Proposition 3 and its parallel in Section 5 show that
24:913:58122:18:18:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this endogenous constraint model is qualitatively consistent with the two key cross-sectional
24:914:58215:18:18:72:200:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:patterns reported earlier.
24:915:58241:18:18:89:539:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Notice that the endogeneity of credit to investment reduces the marginal cost of investing
24:916:58331:18:18:72:539:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for constrained individuals from 1 to 1−κaf′(h) units of current consumption. This should
24:917:58420:18:18:72:540:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:encourage more investment relative to the simple exogenous constraint model. The following
24:918:58510:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:proposition compares the two models at the same level of credit and shows that this is indeed
24:919:58603:18:30:72:116:559:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the case.
24:920:58612:30:18:72:540:529:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f4:F:Proposition 4Fix any(a, w)such thatw < wLmin(a). LethL(a, w)anddL(a, w)denote,
24:921:58690:18:18:72:540:511:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:F:respectively, optimal investment and borrowing in the limited commitment model. Consider
24:922:58778:18:18:72:540:493:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f2:B:the allocations in an exogenous constraint model, whered0=dL(a, w). Then,w < wXmin(a)
24:923:58863:18:35:72:204:475:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:C:andhX(a, w)< hL(a, w).
24:924:58885:35:26:72:368:440:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:4.6  GSL ProgramsPlusPrivate Lenders
24:925:58921:26:17:72:540:414:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We now study the interaction of GSL programs with private sources of financing. To this
24:926:59008:17:18:72:540:397:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:end, we assume that loans from the GSL are fully enforced, while private lenders face limited
24:927:59101:18:18:72:539:379:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:repayment incentives.29This assumption, while strong, is motivated by the fact that GSL
24:928:59188:18:18:72:540:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:programs have much stronger punishments at their disposal than we observe for private
24:929:59273:18:18:72:252:343:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:unsecured lending. (See Section 2.)
24:930:59308:18:18:89:539:325:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:With these two sources of credit, a young individual chooses human capital investments
24:931:59394:18:18:72:539:307:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:B:h, borrowing from the GSLdg, and borrowing from private lendersdpto maximize utility
24:932:59478:18:30:72:315:289:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(1) subject to the sequential budget constraints
24:933:59526:30:21:242:352:259:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:?:c0=w+dg+dp−h,
24:934:59539:21:30:242:368:238:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:?:c1=af[h]−Rdg−Rdp,
24:935:59556:30:17:72:210:208:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the GSL lending guidelines
24:936:59582:17:19:256:355:191:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:A:dg≤min{h, dmax},
24:937:59598:19:16:81:539:172:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:29Alternatively, consider the other extreme in which loans from the GSL face the same limited repayment
24:938:59701:16:12:72:540:156:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:incentives as private loans. In this case, the presence of the GSL program can only make a difference with
24:939:59807:12:12:72:540:144:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:respect to the allocations under limited commitment if some individuals default on GSL loans in equilibrium.
24:940:59915:12:13:72:539:132:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:One can show that the GSL is default-proof ifaL≡Rdmax ̃κf(dmax)is less than or equal to the lower bound for
24:941:60022:13:12:72:539:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the support of ability in the population and if the punishment  ̃κis an upper bound for the elasticity of the
24:942:60131:12:12:72:540:107:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:human capital production function (i.e.f′(h)h/f(h)). In this case, the GSL is completely redundant, and
24:943:60234:12:0:71:358:95:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the allocations coincide with the model with only private lending.
25:944:60302:0:30:72:387:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and the repayment enforcement constraint for private lending
25:945:60362:30:30:271:341:745:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f2:A:dp≤κaf(h) .
25:946:60373:30:18:72:322:715:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We refer to this case with the superscriptG+L.

25:985:62903:31:18:89:539:133:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The implied relationship between human capital investment, ability, and wealth is as
25:986:62987:18:18:72:539:115:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:follows. If ability is low (i.e.a < ̄a), investment equals the unconstrained amount for any

26:988:63161:0:18:72:540:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the rest are constrained. For the latter,hL+G(a, w) is less thanhU(a) and increases with
26:989:63249:18:18:72:540:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:wealth,w. Under the conditions of Proposition 3,hL+G(a, w) is increasing in ability, and
26:990:63337:18:18:72:540:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model with both private lenders and the GSL is qualitatively consistent with investment
26:991:63428:18:18:72:540:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:patterns in the data. The existence of the GSL ensures the unconstrained optimal amount
26:992:63515:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of investment for a broader range of ability and wealth levels, while the existence of private
26:993:63609:18:18:72:540:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lenders delivers the empirically observed positive ability – investment relationship over the
26:994:63702:18:18:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:full distribution of abilities and wealth levels. Both sources of credit play an important role
26:995:63797:18:18:72:209:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in determining investment.
26:996:63823:18:18:89:539:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our preferred model for studying human capital formation includes both public and
26:997:63904:18:18:72:540:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:private sources of lending. First, both sources provide significant credit for higher education
26:998:63999:18:18:72:540:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the U.S. Second, incorporating the combined constraints of GSL programs and private
26:999:64085:18:18:72:540:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lenders in our model produces patterns for investment, ability, and family resources that are
26:1000:64178:18:18:72:540:559:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:qualitatively consistent with U.S. data. Moreover, as we now show, a calibrated model with
26:1001:64268:18:40:72:333:541:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:both sources of credit performs well quantitatively.
26:1002:64320:40:29:72:288:501:17:g_d0_f1:17:g_d0_f1:C:5  A Quantitative Model
26:1003:64343:29:18:72:540:472:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we extend our framework to a multi-period setting and incorporate education
26:1004:64435:18:18:72:540:454:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:subsidies in order to explore the quantitative implications of alternative forms of borrow-
26:1005:64526:18:18:72:540:436:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ing constraints. After calibrating the model to match the U.S. economy, we compare the
26:1006:64612:18:18:72:540:418:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:predicted cross-sectional patterns for investment, ability, and wealth under different assump-
26:1007:64706:18:18:72:540:400:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tions about credit constraints. We also simulate an increase in both the returns to and costs
26:1008:64799:18:18:72:540:382:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of schooling (as observed over the 1980s and 1990s in the U.S.) in order to see whether our
26:1009:64890:18:18:72:540:364:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model with public and private lending can explain the rising importance of family resources
26:1010:64981:18:18:72:540:346:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(as a determinant of schooling) and private lending for college. Overall, the model performs
26:1011:65073:18:34:72:452:328:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:well when compared against the empirical patterns discussed in Section 3.
26:1012:65146:34:26:72:186:294:14:g_d0_f1:14:g_d0_f1:C:5.1  The Model
26:1013:65160:26:18:72:540:268:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Consider individuals with a lifespan of lengthT >1. As of anyt0∈[0, T], the utility of an

26:1025:65384:18:18:71:94:179:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:rate.
26:1026:65389:18:18:89:540:161:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Life is divided into three stages: “Youth”,t∈[0,1], when individuals attend school and
26:1027:65475:18:18:72:539:143:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:do not work; “maturity,”t∈[1, P], when they participate full-time in labor markets; and
26:1028:65562:18:18:72:516:125:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:“retirement,”t∈[P, T], when they do neither and consume from accumulated savings.
26:1029:65643:18:0:89:539:107:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Att= 0, individuals are endowed with financial assetsw≥0 and an ability levela >0.
27:1030:65727:0:18:72:540:775:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Initial resources,w, can be seen as the present value of family transfers.30Ability,a, reflects
27:1031:65822:18:18:71:539:757:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:genetic traits, early educational investments, and other individual characteristics that may
27:1032:65914:18:18:71:261:739:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:determine the returns on investment.
27:1033:65950:18:18:89:540:721:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:For simplicity, we assume that the market interest rate equalsρ, so individuals prefer
27:1034:66036:18:18:72:540:703:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:flat lifecycle consumption profiles when unconstrained. While we explicitly model different
27:1035:66127:18:18:72:540:685:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:forms of credit constraints that apply during the “youth” investment period, we assume
27:1036:66213:18:18:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that credit markets are frictionless once individuals enter the labor market.31This greatly
27:1037:66304:18:18:72:540:649:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:simplifies the analysis and allows us to focus on the effects of constraints most directly
27:1038:66394:18:18:72:540:631:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:related to investment decisions (and to tightly link this lifecycle framework with that of our
27:1039:66488:18:18:72:202:613:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:two-period model above).
27:1040:66512:18:18:89:539:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Government subsidizes schooling in two ways. First, it provides all young persons with
27:1041:66598:18:18:72:539:577:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a free-of-charge investment flowipub≥0. Second, it matches every privately financed unit

27:1055:67203:18:18:72:540:337:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:time during “youth”.  To simplify the exposition, and to abstract from tangential issues

27:1059:67550:18:18:72:539:266:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:expressions below. Finally,g≥0 is the rate of return to labor market experience (or the
27:1060:67637:18:18:72:309:248:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:rate of growth in earnings over the lifecycle).33
27:1061:67686:18:15:81:539:230:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:30For simplicity of exposition, we will assume that human capital is produced from goods rather than
27:1062:67786:15:12:72:540:215:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:time inputs. We could equivalently assume that human capital investment only requires time inputs and
27:1063:67887:12:12:72:540:203:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:that an individual’s total ‘initial wealth’,w, reflects family transfers plus the total discounted value of
27:1064:67994:12:12:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:earnings he could receive if he worked (rather than attended school) full-time during “youth”. In this case,
27:1065:68102:12:12:72:540:179:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:private investment costs reflect any earnings foregone for school. Our calibration below implicitly assumes
27:1066:68209:12:12:72:540:167:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:both goods and time investments are perfectly substitutable and combines these costs to determine total
27:1067:68312:12:8:72:199:155:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:investment in human capital.
27:1068:68340:8:12:81:433:147:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:?:31Cameron and Taber (2004) make an analogous assumption in their framework.
27:1069:68415:12:16:81:539:135:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:32Given our assumptions below, the timing of ‘free’ investment will be irrelevant – only the total discounted
27:1070:68524:16:8:72:481:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:value of all ‘free’ investment matters. All investment not provided free is subsidized at rates.
27:1071:68620:8:16:81:539:111:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:33Our main theoretical results extend to the case wheregis increasing ina(i.e. more able individuals have
